                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanotubes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Carbon%20nanotubes">Carbon nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Binding+Energy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_binding%20energy">binding energy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Expanded+Interlayer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_expanded%20interlayer">expanded interlayer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Intercalation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_intercalation">intercalation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Partially+Expanded&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_partially%20expanded">partially expanded</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Large scale energy storage technology plays a significant role in the integration of renewable energy resources with the grid power supply. Lithium ion battery (LIB) technology is considered as a key device among all energy storage devices due to its high energy density and long cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Despite these advantages, lithium ion batteries have failed to meet the needs of large scale energy storage systems due to the uneven distribution of lithium resources and consequent problems in importing the lithium resources leading to an increase in cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> As the sodium ion belongs to the alkaline family, the similar physical and chemical properties of sodium to lithium (redox potential −2.7 V for Na/Na<sup>+</sup> vs standard hydrogen electrode (SHE) and −3.02 V for Li/Li<sup>+</sup> vs SHE) draw significant attention and sodium can be one of the most promising alternatives to lithium-based storage technology. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> The high abundancy of sodium in the earth’s crust makes sodium ion batteries appealing to be a cost-effective storage technology for large scale applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Though sodium metal has a high theoretical specific capacity of 1166 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, sodium metal batteries, which work on the mechanism of sodium metal stripping and plating, face the problem of dendrite formation. Though many research groups are working to minimize the dendrite formation, the commercialization of sodium metal batteries is difficult due to safety concern. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> So, the sodium ion battery is considered to be a promising alternative battery technology.</div><div class="NLM_p">However, it remains a great challenge for the sodium ion battery (NIB) to achieve a comparable performance to the LIB due to its larger ionic radius (1.07 Å for Na<sup>+</sup> and 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>) and slow reaction kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> In addition, the larger size of the sodium ions affects the mass transport properties and electrochemical storage and causes a huge volume expansion. These negative effects restrict the adaptability of the current satisfactory LIB electrode materials to NIB technology. For instance, graphite is an intercalation-based anode material used in commercial LIBs and it delivers a high reversible specific capacity of 372 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> in the operating voltage from 0.1 to 0.2 V vs Li/Li<sup>+</sup>. Unfortunately, the specific capacity of graphite for the insertion of the sodium ions is limited due to the thermodynamic consideration, wherein sodium ions form plating on the surface of the anode before being intercalated into graphite. The sodium intercalated graphite forms NaC<sub>64</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> rather than NaC<sub>6</sub>, whereas in lithium it forms stage-I intercalated compound LiC<sub>6</sub>; therefore, the reversible specific capacity obtained for graphite in a sodium ion battery is limited to 30 mAhg<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 and 11">(9,11)</a> This is in accordance with the theoretical results that sodium intercalation/deintercalation is possible in the carbon structures only with a minimum interlayer spacing of 0.37 nm. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Different strategies have been adopted to increase the sodium ion storage in graphite. A technique was employed, wherein diglyme-based solvent molecules were co-intercalated into the graphite structure to achieve an increased interlayer spacing and it exhibited a stable reversible capacity of 100 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> with a potential plateau of 0.6 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Though reversible capacity can be obtained by this method, the primary obstacle lies in the huge volume expansion resulting from the process of intercalation and deintercalation, which limits its commercialization. Therefore, it is necessary to find a suitable host material to accommodate and transport Na<sup>+</sup> ions. So far, various materials like carbonaceous materials <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> as well as metal- and metal oxide-based systems <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 12 to 17">(12−17)</a> are explored as an anode in sodium ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The conversion and alloying-based materials like metals and metal oxides exhibit a huge volume expansion, which leads to rapid capacity fading. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> Among these materials, carbon-based electrode materials are considered as dominant electrode materials in the energy storage technology due to its good electrical conductivity with better electrochemical properties and cost effectiveness. To overcome the various challenges faced by NIB technology, many other carbon-based materials like hard carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> soft carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> porous carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> hollow carbon nanowires, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> and expanded graphite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> have been used as an anode material in a sodium ion battery in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. Unfortunately, a low Coulombic efficiency and poor cyclic stability remains an obstacle for the use of carbon-based materials. Yang et al. reported expanded graphite as a sodium ion battery anode by the process of partial oxidation and reduction of graphite, and it delivers a specific capacity of 284 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Here the rate capability of the electrode is not satisfactory due to the low electrical conductivity of the expanded graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Yun et al. have investigated reduced graphene oxide as an anode for a sodium ion battery, which delivers a specific capacity of about 174 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 40 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Though a better rate capability is obtained for reduced graphene oxide, the specific capacity obtained is very low. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Nanostructured one-dimensional materials like nanowires and nanotubes provide new opportunities to improve the properties of energy storage materials for various batteries due to its good electrical conductivity and structural stability. Yuliang et al. employed a one-dimensional hollow carbon nanowire as an anode in a sodium ion battery, and the specific capacity obtained is 251 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which offers a better specific capacity than two-dimensional carbon-based materials. The enhanced specific capacity is due to an increased interlayer spacing of 0.37 nm, which is also proved by theoretical studies. Though a high specific capacity can be obtained, the synthesis technique has been carried out at a very high temperature, which increases the production cost of materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> One-dimensional carbon nanostructures like multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) have a good electrical conductivity with an interlayer spacing of 0.34 nm. MWCNTs are considered as an excellent active material for lithium ion storage because they can store lithium ions similar to graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Moreover, due to the higher aspect ratio, MWCNTs can deliver a higher capacity than graphite. Due to their higher electrical conductivity, MWCNTs have been used as a conductive support as well as a buffering matrix for controlling the volume expansion of alloy-type metal and metal oxides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> Though MWCNTs have a good electrical conductivity, the storage of sodium ions is limited due to the interlayer spacing. Multiwalled carbon nanotubes have been used as an anode in sodium ion batteries, where the specific capacity obtained is around 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> It was concluded from this work that MWCNTs are unfavorable for the insertion of sodium ions due to their insufficient d spacing and low active sites. This limited capacity hinders the use of MWCNTs as a high capacity anode material in a sodium ion battery.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this aspect, we have designed to expand only a few outer layers of the MWCNT with the inner core of the MWCNT remaining undisturbed using Hummer’s method. Hummer’s method offers a better advantage compared to other techniques in unzipping the outer few layers. This helps to obtain partial unzipping of only a few outer layers of the MWCNT without causing any damage to the inner core of the MWCNT. However, in other methods like the use of strong acids and other techniques, complete opening of the MWCNT to form a graphene nanoribbon is obtained. Also, by other methods, there is a possibility to damage the inner morphology of the MWCNT; thereby, it affects the electrical conductivity of the sample. With the modified structure, the interlayer spacing of the outer few layers gets expanded to 0.8 nm due to the intercalation of functional groups. The interlayer expanded outer few layers offer more sites for the insertion of sodium ions as well as more active sodium ion adsorption sites, and the undisturbed inner core offers a good electrical conductivity, which helps to attain both a better rate capability and high specific capacity. A comparison with MWCNTs employed as an anode material is conducted. Further investigations on the enhancement in capacity are studied using the DFTB molecular dynamics-based theoretical calculation. Also, the binding energy of sodium ions with a PECNT is determined using DFT calculations, where the larger exothermic nature of the binding energy of a sodium atom favors the formation of stable bonding with the PECNT and thus delivers a high specific capacity.</div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i2" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i2"> Results and Discussion</h3><div class="NLM_p">The crystalline structure and the phase purity were obtained using the X-ray diffraction technique (XRD). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the XRD pattern of the MWCNT and PECNT. The intense peak observed at 26.2° in the MWCNT corresponds to the C (002) hexagonal plane of graphite with an interlayer spacing of 0.34 nm. The intense peak is due to the long-range order and high crystallinity of the MWCNT. The signals at 43° and 54° correspond to the (100) and (002) planes, respectively. The introduction of functional groups into the graphitic carbon materials causes an increase in the interlayer spacing from 0.34 nm to a higher value depending on the quantity of functional groups attached. In the PECNT, along with the graphitic peak C(002) centered at 25.8°, another peak is observed at 9.23°, corresponding to the C (002) plane. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The shift in C (002) to a lower angle strongly suggests that the introduction of oxygen functional groups is into the outer layers of the carbon nanotubes, while with the inner core of the carbon nanotubes remains undisturbed. The intense peak centered at 25.8° with a broadening in the peak from 20 to 35° confirms the unraveled outer layers of the MWCNT. Thus, it again confirms that during the oxidation process only a few outer layers get oxidized, leaving the inner layers intact. Further, the opening of the outer layers of the MWCNT can be quantified from the ratio of the intensity of the peak centered at 9.23° to the sum of the intensities of the peaks centered at 25.8° and 9.23°, and the corresponding ratio is determined to be 0.25. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> This signifies a partial opening of the multiwalled carbon nanotubes.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0005.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of the MWCNT and PECNT. (b) FTIR spectra of the MWCNT and PECNT. (c) TGA curves of the MWCNT and PECNT. (d) Raman spectra of the MWCNT and PECNT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The molecular vibrational characteristics were determined by the Raman spectroscopy technique. The Raman spectra of the MWCNT and PECNT are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. Three major peaks corresponding to D band, G band, and 2D are observed at 1341, 1558, and 2689 cm<sup>–1</sup> for the MWCNT and 1333, 1563, and 2685 cm<sup>–1</sup> for the PECNT, respectively. The G band is due to the sp<sup>2</sup> bond stretching of the E<sub>2g</sub> mode, the D band is due to the structural imperfections present in the carbon basal plane and at the edge sites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> and the 2D band is used to define the quality of the graphene. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio defines the degree of disorder present in the carbon nanostructures, and this ratio for the PECNT increases to 0.982 from 0.65 as in the case of the MWCNT. A low intensity D band is observed in the MWCNT, which arises mainly due to the defects created during the process of acid purification to remove the catalyst. The introduction of functional groups in the PECNT causes a decrease in the average size of the sp<sup>2</sup> domain after the oxidation process and thus leads to an increase in the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio. Moreover, an upshift in the G band is observed in the PECNT, which is ascribed to the shortening of the C–C bond due to the electron transfer from the MWCNT to the functional groups and is an indication of intercalation of functional groups in the PECNT. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analysis was carried out to determine the thermal stability of the material as well as to confirm the presence of functional groups. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, the MWCNT shows a significant weight loss above 700 °C, which is due to the decomposition of multiwalled carbon nanotubes. In the case of the PECNT, an initial weight loss around 100 °C is due to the removal of water molecules, and upon an increase in the temperature, weight loss occurs between 150 and 250 °C. This is due to the formation of CO and CO<sub>2</sub> gases, which are released as the result of the easily removable functional groups attached on the surface of the PECNT. Weight loss between 250 and 650 °C indicates the removal of more stable oxygen-containing functional groups, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> and the decomposition of the PECNT occurs beyond 650 °C. From the TGA curve, it can be inferred that around 15 wt % of functional groups are present in the PECNT. Moreover, the thermal stability of the PECNT is reduced when compared to the MWCNT as the introduction of oxygen functional groups weakens the van der Waals forces between the layers and also disrupts the hexagonal carbon basal plane, accelerating the process of weight loss. To evaluate the presence of different functional groups, the FTIR measurement was carried out as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. During the process of oxidation using H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and KMnO<sub>4</sub>, the carboxylic, epoxy, and hydroxyl functional groups are attached on the surface of the MWCNT. This process creates defects and stretches the C—C bond, leading to an opening of the few outer layers of the MWCNT. The presence of a broad and intense peak as observed in the PECNT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) from 3200 to 3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to OH stretching. A weak OH stretching peak is also observed in the MWCNT because of the attachment of a few functional groups during the purification process of the MWCNT. The PECNT shows signals at 1049 and 1408 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to carboxyl and epoxy functional groups, respectively, which confirms the presence of COOH groups. Also, an intense peak present at 1690 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to the carboxylic C═O group. The peaks arising at 2920 and 2845 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the symmetric and asymmetric vibrations of the CH<sub><i>x</i></sub> group. These functional groups are not prominent in the case of the MWCNT, which confirms the structure of the MWCNT is not attached with any functional groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms are used to determine the specific surface area and porosity of the MWCNT and PECNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The porosity and specific surface area are interpreted using the Brunauer–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) and Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) methods, respectively. The BET isotherms for the MWCNT and PECNT are done at an atmospheric pressure and temperature and follows the type IV isotherm. This indicates the presence of relatively large pores in the sample. The BET surface area of the PECNT (124 m<sup>2</sup>/g) is much higher than that of the MWCNT (60 m<sup>2</sup>/g). The surface area enhancement also serves as evidence for the successful opening of the outer layers of the MWCNT. As seen from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, the adsorption of nitrogen in the PECNT is higher when compared to the MWCNT even at a low pressure. This is due to the fact that the opening of carbon nanotubes by the oxygen functional groups induces defects and pores on the surface of the carbon nanotubes, which help in adsorption. The desorption pore volume as a function of pore width is calculated using the BJH method, and the distribution of pore size is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, where the maximum pore diameter is centered at 2.4 nm, indicating the mesoporous structure. This facilitates a faster diffusion of sodium ions into the PECNT when compared to the MWCNT.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphological structure of the MWCNT and PECNT was investigated using the transmission electron microscopy technique. The typical TEM images of the MWCNT and PECNT are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. The morphology of the MWCNT shows a smooth outer surface with a curved and entangled structure and has an average outer diameter in the range of 60 nm and length in the range of micrometers. After the process of oxidation, it is clearly observed that the MWCNT undergoes a morphology transformation. The surface of the PECNT looks like a distorted graphene nanoribbon covered over the surface of the carbon nanotubes. It is clearly observed from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d that only a few outer layers of the carbon nanotubes are unzipped successfully along the longitudinal direction of the multiwalled carbon nanotubes yielding shorter graphene fragments. The length as well as inner core of the carbon nanotubes still could be identified; this thereby helps in maintaining the conductivity of the carbon nanotubes. This is in good agreement with the XRD data as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. This unique feature thus helps to achieve additional sodium ion storage sites as well as aids in achieving a good rate capability.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0006.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM images of the (a, b) MWCNT and (c, d) PECNT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The XPS technique was carried out to understand the nature of functional groups attached to the PECNT. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> represents the XPS spectrum of the PECNT. The survey spectrum shows only the presence of carbon and oxygen, and there are no additional metal impurities present in the sample. After deconvolution and fitting, five peaks are centered at 284.2, 285.1, 286, 287.1, and 288.7 eV corresponding to the C—C sp<sup>2</sup> carbon, sp<sup>3</sup> carbon, C—OH, C—O—C, and O—C═O, respectively. This indicates the attachment of functional groups on the surface of the PECNT. To understand the electrochemical property of the MWCNT and PECNT, these materials were used as electrodes in half-cell experiments. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows the initial three CV curves of the MWCNT and PECNT that are recorded at scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the potential range from 0.01 to 2.8 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Two major reduction peaks are observed at 0.42 V and at 0.01 V in the PECNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>) and one reduction peak at 0.01 V in the CNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>) in the first cycle. The reduction peak in the first cycle is due to the decomposition of the electrolyte to form a solid electrolyte interface on the surface of the electrode. The reduction peak at 0.42 V is due to the irreversible reaction of functional groups present on the surface of the PECNT with an electrolyte. There is no obvious reduction peak at 0.42 V in the MWCNT, which confirms the absence of functional groups on the surface of the MWCNT. The disappearance of the irreversible cathodic peak in the next cycles confirms the formation of the stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. Also, the reduction peak observed at 0.01 V is due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the carbon structure as well as the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions on the active surface of the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The cathodic peak current at 0.01 V is observed to be higher in the PECNT when compared to the MWCNT, which is ascribed to the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage. The enhanced sodium ion storage in the PECNT is mainly contributed from the expanded interlayers by the functional groups, which provides more active sites for the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the carbon structure. Additionally, the presence of rectangular shaped curves indicates that the storage mechanism is also contributed by the capacitive behavior, i.e., physical adsorption of sodium ions.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the electrochemical behavior, galvanostatic charge–discharge studies were carried out in the potential range of 0.01 to 2.8 V at different current densities. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows the charge–discharge profile cycled at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In the initial cycle, the MWCNT shows a discharge capacity of 597 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a charge capacity of 179 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency of 29.9%. The PECNT shows an initial discharge capacity of 1095 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a charge capacity of 342 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency of 31.2%. The first cycle Coulombic efficiency depends on the irreversible formation of the SEI layer, which in turn depends on the surface area of the active material. Since the surface area of the PECNT is high compared to hard carbon, the first cycle Coulombic efficiency is low. In further cycles, the Coulombic efficiency is close to 100%, which confirms the formation of a stable SEI layer in the initial cycle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> The decomposition of propylene carbonate in the presence of PVDF binder leads to the formation of the thicker SEI layer. This results in a low initial Coulombic efficiency. The initial Coulombic efficiency may be improved by using other types of electrolyte solvents like ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate or ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate. In addition, the use of additives in the electrolyte can also improve the Coulombic efficiency. The charge–discharge studies were carried out for different current densities, and the profile shows a sloping and a plateau region for both the PECNT and MWCNT as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b. As reported by previous studies on sodium storage in hard carbon and soft carbon, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the high voltage region corresponds to the insertion of sodium ions into graphene layers and at a low voltage corresponds to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into pores and defects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> This signifies that the capacity contributed from the high voltage region (&gt;0.7 V) is due to the insertion of sodium ions into the graphitic layers in the PECNT, and in the low voltage region (&lt;0.7 V), the capacity contribution is due to the adsorption of sodium ions into the pores and defects. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, the capacity contribution from the sloping region up to 0.7 V is 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the case of the PECNT and 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the case of the MWCNT at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. This further confirms that intercalation of sodium ions is facilitated in the case of the PECNT rather than the MWCNT. The synergistic advantage of an enlarged d spacing in the PECNT facilitates the transport and accommodation of more Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the graphitic layers as well as the partial opening of the PECNT into few layer graphene nanoribbons helps to adsorb more sodium ions, which in turn helps to achieve an enhanced specific capacity. The complete opening of the MWCNT into graphene nanoribbons helps to adsorb more of sodium ions, but simultaneously, it causes the problem of restacking during the extended charging and discharging cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The synergistic role of the inner core and outer graphene nanoribbons helps to achieve simultaneously a good specific capacity and electrical conductivity due to the inner core.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Charge–discharge curves of the PECNT at different current densities. (b) Charge–discharge curves of the MWCNT at different current densities. (c) Rate capability of the PECNT and MWCNT. (d) Cyclic stability of the PECNT and MWCNT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability measurements of the MWCNT and PECNT were performed at different current densities from 20 to 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. When cycled at different current densities, a better rate capabilities with technologically significant specific capacities of 510, 249, 165, 116, and 67 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 20, 50, 100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, are obtained for the PECNT. The MWCNT shows a specific capacity of 222, 105, 66, 26, and 1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 20, 50, 100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The PECNT shows a specific capacity, which is about 2.3 times the specific capacity obtained for the MWCNT. This enhancement in capacity is contributed mainly by the increased interlayer spacing, which facilitates the transport and accommodation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions; the high specific surface area offered by the opening of carbon nanotubes facilitates a faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport even at a higher current density. When cycled back to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the PECNT shows a capacity retention of more than 80% and thus demonstrates the stability of the PECNT over wide charge–discharge conditions. The PECNT exhibits a better rate capability when compared to hard carbon in the previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref21 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref21 ref41" aria-label="reference 12, 21 and 41">(12,21,41)</a> The long-term cyclic stability was carried out at a low current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 10 cycles and then at a high current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 100 cycles as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. The PECNT also exhibits a satisfactory cycling stability at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a specific capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, while the MWCNT shows 40 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the reaction kinetics, the cyclic voltammetry was carried out at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and the graph is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>. The maximum anodic peak currents as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a> are plotted as a function of square root of the scan rate. Their linear relationship shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a> indicates the diffusion-controlled reaction where the Randles–Sevcik equation is applicable:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> is the peak current, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>D</i> is the diffusion coefficient, <i>C</i> is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the electrode material, and <i>v</i> is the scan rate. The linear relationship between peak current and square root of the scan rate indicates that the process is a diffusion-controlled process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c,d</a>). Also, the plot of logarithmic scan rate versus logarithmic peak current as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> indicates the linear relationship. The linear fitting of the MWCNT gives a slope of 0.9, which indicates that the process is a surface capacitive process. The slope of the PECNT gives a value of 0.6, indicating the diffusion-controlled process. Also, to evaluate the capacity contribution from the diffusion-controlled reaction and surface capacitive process, CV curves at different scan rates like 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are done as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a,b</a>. According to Dunn, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> the proportion of the sodium ion capacity contribution can be quantified by separating the current response at a certain potential into the capacitive or surface-controlled reaction (proportional to scan rate (<i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled reaction (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>)):<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>For analytical purposes, the equation is rearranged to<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>By determining the constants <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>, the fraction of the current from surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled reactions can be determined. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The quantification indicates that for the PECNT at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> the diffusion-controlled reaction contributes to about 63% and surface-controlled reaction contributes to about 37% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c</a>). This indicates that majority of the capacity originated from the intercalation of sodium ions, but in the case of MWCNT at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the majority of the capacity originated from the surface-controlled process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7d</a>). This again confirms the ease of intercalation of sodium ions into the PECNT rather than the MWCNT. In order to understand the interaction between sodium atoms and the MWCNT, a density functional tight binding (DFTB) molecular dynamics simulation is done at 300 K. At first, a 1261 atom, double-walled carbon nanotube (DWCNT) is constructed using an inner CNT (5,5) and outer CNT (10,10) as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_002.avi" class="ext-link">Movie SM1</a>, where the distance between two CNTs is 3.39 Å. This atomistic structure is smaller compared to the experimental structure. As the DFTB calculations are more accurate and computationally heavier than classical molecular dynamics results, we have adopted these structures in order to study the microscopic details of the Na atom interaction with the PECNT. Moreover, the realistic movement of sodium atoms inside the PECNT is given by the molecular dynamics calculation, which takes care of the temperature of the environment. At first, the sodium atom is kept at the edge of the DWCNT, and the Langevin molecular dynamics simulation is carried out at 300 K. Even though sodium interacts well with carbon atoms in the CNT, it is not able to enter the gap between the outer and inner CNTs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_002.avi" class="ext-link">Movie SM1</a>). This is consistent with the experimental results of a lower specific capacity of the MWCNT. To understand the sodium atom interaction with the PECNT, we created a partially opened DWCNT with carboxylic functional groups and hydrogen atom termination as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. We carried out the DFTB Langevin molecular dynamics simulation at 300 K by keeping the sodium atom inside the partially opened DWCNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_003.avi" class="ext-link">Movies SM2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_004.avi" class="ext-link">SM3</a>). Radial distribution function is plotted between the sodium and the carbon atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The distribution function shows a pronounced peak at 2.77 Å, indicating that this is the dominant nearest-neighbor distance between the sodium and the carbon atoms at room temperature. The pronounced peak indicates the stable binding characteristics of a sodium atom in the PECNT. Furthermore, molecular trajectories of a sodium atom do not show any rapid movement of the sodium atom (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_003.avi" class="ext-link">Movies SM2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_004.avi" class="ext-link">SM3</a>). Sodium is caged between the hexagonal rings in the outer and inner DWCNTs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_005.avi" class="ext-link">Movie SM4</a>), which leads to a stable binding of the sodium atom to the PECNT.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Geometrically optimized atomistic structure of the partially unzipped MWCNT–sodium complex, (a) front view and (b) side view. Gray, red, light-blue, and purple balls are carbon, oxygen, hydrogen, and sodium atoms, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the stability of the sodium atom binding to the PECNT, the binding energy is calculated based on the density functional theory (DFT) simulation. In the binding energy calculation, we have to account for the van der Waals interactions properly, which are underestimated by local and semilocal exchange-correlation functionals. In our calculation, the long-range van der Waals interaction is included through the semiempirical corrections based on Grimme D2. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The geometrically optimized atomistic structure of the PECNT and sodium complex is given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The binding energy of the sodium atom is calculated based on the following equation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Here, <i>E</i><sub>MWCNT+Na</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>MWCNT</sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> are the total energies of the PECNT and sodium complex, PECNT, and sodium atom, respectively. The calculated binding energy is −1.50 eV. Based on <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>, this larger negative value of binding energy indicates a more favorable exothermic reaction occurring between the PECNT and the sodium atom. Even though binding energy is much higher than thermal energy, they do not form chemical bonding. This binding energy is larger than sodium-pristine graphene and sodium-pristine MWCNT (5,5) complexes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/suppl_file/nl8b02275_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), which are calculated to be −1.17 and −1.22 eV, respectively. In general, this is consistent with our experimental results. For further analysis, the bonding nature of sodium atom with the MWCNT, density of states (DOS), wave function, and electron difference density analysis are carried out (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). The projected DOS of the sodium atom does not any show a strong state around the Fermi-energy; the peak at −0.11 eV is the next closer state (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). This state shows that there is very weak overlap of wave function between the sodium atom and MWCNT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). Furthermore, the electron difference density of the entire PECNT–sodium complex indicates no appreciable charge mixing between the sodium atom and MWCNT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). These analyses clearly manifest that the sodium atom is not chemically bonded to the PECNT, which makes it suitable for a sodium ion battery anode.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/medium/nl-2018-02275w_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Total DOS plot of the PECNT–sodium complex. Inset: projected DOS plot of a sodium atom. Around the Fermi-level, the Na atom has a state at −0.11 eV. Fermi-level is aligned to 0 eV in these plots. (b) Wave function plot of the PECNT–sodium complex at −0.11 eV. Isovalue: 0.005. The color of the wave function indicates the phase of the wave function. (c) Electron difference density plot of the PECNT–sodium complex.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275/asset/images/large/nl-2018-02275w_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Conclusion</h3><div class="NLM_p">In summary, the expansion of a few outer layers in multiwall carbon nanotubes by functional groups has been successfully obtained by Hummer’s method and confirmed by XRD, FTIR, and TGA analyses. The partial opening of a few outer layers in the MWCNT facilitates the intercalation of sodium ions into the carbon structure and also provides sufficient active sites for the adsorption of sodium ions due to its increased surface area. The inner core of the PECNT remains undisturbed, which helps to maintain the electrical conductivity, and is evident from the better rate capability. The van der Waals interaction corrected DFT calculations showed exothermic binding energy of −1.50 eV in the sodium–PECNT complex compared to −1.17 and −1.22 eV in sodium-pristine graphene and sodium-pristine MWCNT (5,5) complexes, respectively. This larger binding energy confirms the enhancement in the specific capacity of the PECNT compared to the pristine MWCNT. Energy levels, wave function, and charge density analyses show that this binding is physisorption in nature. The larger negative value of binding energy favors the bonding between the PECNT and sodium atom, which helps to enhance the electrochemical performance. The proof of concept reported here will be beneficial for the design and production of a rechargeable sodium ion battery with the PECNT as a promising anode candidate for sodium ion batteries.</div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Method of Preparation of PECNT</h4><div class="NLM_p">The MWCNT was synthesized by the catalytic chemical vapor deposition technique, where misch metal and nickel-based AB<sub>3</sub> alloy were used as the catalyst for the growth of the MWCNT. The growth of the MWCNT was carried out at 700 °C in an argon atmosphere using acetylene as the carbon precursor. As obtained, the MWCNT was further air oxidized at 450 °C and acid treated with HNO<sub>3</sub> to remove amorphous carbon and metal particles, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">The PECNT was prepared by Hummer’s method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Briefly, the calculated quantity of the MWCNT was obtained in concentrated H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> for 5 min at ice temperature. NaNO<sub>3</sub> was added very slowly to the above mixture, followed by the addition of KMnO<sub>4</sub>. The ratio of MWCNT/KMnO<sub>4</sub> was maintained in a certain ratio in order to obtain the controlled oxidization of the outer few layers, leaving the inner core of the MWCNT intact. Warm water (50 °C) was added very slowly to the reaction mixture, followed by the addition of hydrogen peroxide. The addition of hydrogen peroxide removes the unreacted compounds formed in the process. Also, to completely remove the byproducts of the reaction, the sample is immersed in DI water and decanted repeatedly until the pH of the solution become neutral. Then the solution is filtered and washed several times with DI water to ensure the removal of all impurities present. The presence of functional groups causes the partial opening of the MWCNT.</div></div><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Material Characterization</h4><div class="NLM_p last">Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded using a Rigaku Smartlab X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5418 Å). All measurements were taken in the range from 10° to 90° with the step size of 0.02°. Raman spectra were recorded using a WiTec Alpha Raman spectrometer in the range from 500 to 3000 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The laser source used was Nd/YAG (532 nm). Thermogravimetric analysis was carried out in an SDTQ600 analyzer from TA Instruments in air to 1000 °C at a heating rate of 20 °C/min with a flow rate of 160 mL/min. The surface area and pore size analysis were done using the Micromeritics ASAP 2020 instrument using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) theory. In order to study the morphology of the samples, high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) images were recorded with a Technai G20 instrument by drop casting the sample solution in ethanol on holey carbon-coated 200 mesh copper grids. A PerkinElmer FTIR spectrometer was used to record the FTIR spectrum of samples.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The electrode was fabricated by a slurry coating process. The slurry was prepared by mixing 75% active material and 10% acetylene black with 15% binder polyvinylidene difluoride in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. The working electrodes were prepared by coating the slurry on copper foil, which were then dried at 80 °C overnight in a vacuum oven. The dried electrodes were cut in the form of a circular disc of 12 mm. The mass loading of the active material is 1 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>; 2032-coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox where the oxygen and moisture levels were maintained below 0.1 ppm using sodium metal as the counter electrode and reference electrode and a glass fiber membrane (Whatman GF/C) as the separator. The electrolyte used was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC). The cells were galvanostatically cycled at various current densities between 0.01 and 2.8 V on a Biologic BCS 810 battery cycler. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was carried out on the Biologic SP 300. The cyclic voltammetry was carried out in the potential range from 0.01 to 2.8 V at the scan rate of 0.5 mV/s for 5 cycles.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Computational Method</h4><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, all DFT calculations are performed using the density functional theory (DFT) code Quantumwise ATK, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a>which is based on a linear combination of numerical atomic orbitals and norm-conserving Troullier–Martins pseudopotentials. The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof exchange-correlation functional, which is derived within the generalized gradient approximation (GGA), is used with semiempirical corrections based on Grimme D2 for the van der Waals interaction. Double zeta plus polarized basis sets are used, and calculations are carried out with the supercells shown in the corresponding figures. In order to closely resemble the experimental works, all of the edge atoms of the MWCNT are terminated with hydrogen atoms and carboxylic groups. To avoid any spurious interactions with neighbor super cells, a minimum vacuum distance of 10 Å was kept between the adjacent MWCNT in all of the electronic calculations. DFTB calculations are done using Slate–Koster parameters <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> implemented in Quantumwise ATK.</div></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div         </p><ol id="references" class="useLabel"><li id="ref1"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">1</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit1" data-doi="10.1021/ja3091438"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J. B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K.</span></span> <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>135</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1167</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1176</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja3091438</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DGoodenough%252C%2BJ.%2BB.%253B%2BPark%252C%2BK.%2BJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%2B2013%252C%2B135%252C%2B1167%25E2%2580%25931176%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja3091438&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Li-Ion Rechargeable Battery: A Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.; Park, Kyu-Sung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1167-1176</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Each cell of a battery stores elec. energy as chem. energy in two electrodes, a reductant (anode) and an oxidant (cathode), sepd. by an electrolyte that transfers the ionic component of the chem. reaction inside the cell and forces the electronic component outside the battery.  The output on discharge is an external electronic current I at a voltage V for a time Δt.  The chem. reaction of a rechargeable battery must be reversible on the application of a charging I and V.  Crit. parameters of a rechargeable battery are safety, d. of energy that can be stored at a specific power input and retrieved at a specific power output, cycle and shelf life, storage efficiency, and cost of fabrication.  Conventional ambient-temp. rechargeable batteries have solid electrodes and a liq. electrolyte.  The pos. electrode (cathode) consists of a host framework into which the mobile (working) cation is inserted reversibly over a finite solid-soln. range.  The solid-soln. range, which is reduced at higher current by the rate of transfer of the working ion across electrode/electrolyte interfaces and within a host, limits the amt. of charge per electrode formula unit that can be transferred over the time Δt = Δt(I).  Moreover, the difference between energies of the LUMO and the HOMO of the electrolyte, i.e., electrolyte window, dets. the max. voltage for a long shelf and cycle life.  The max. stable voltage with an aq. electrolyte is 1.5 V; the Li-ion rechargeable battery uses an org. electrolyte with a larger window, which increase the d. of stored energy for a given Δt.  Anode or cathode electrochem. potentials outside the electrolyte window can increase V, but they require formation of a passivating surface layer that must be permeable to Li+ and capable of adapting rapidly to the changing electrode surface area as the electrode changes vol. during cycling.  A passivating surface layer adds to the impedance of the Li+ transfer across the electrode/electrolyte interface and lowers the cycle life of a battery cell.  Moreover, formation of a passivation layer on the anode robs Li from the cathode irreversibly on an initial charge, further lowering the reversible Δt.  These problems plus the cost of quality control of manufg. plague development of Li-ion rechargeable batteries that can compete with the internal combustion engine for powering elec. cars and that can provide the needed low-cost storage of elec. energy generated by renewable wind and/or solar energy.  Chemists are contributing to incremental improvements of the conventional strategy by investigating and controlling electrode passivation layers, improving the rate of Li+ transfer across electrode/electrolyte interfaces, identifying electrolytes with larger windows while retaining a Li+ cond. σLi &gt; 10-3 S cm-1, synthesizing electrode morphologies that reduce the size of the active particles while pinning them on current collectors of large surface area accessible by the electrolyte, lowering the cost of cell fabrication, designing displacement-reaction anodes of higher capacity that allow a safe, fast charge, and designing alternative cathode hosts.  However, new strategies are needed for batteries that go beyond powering hand-held devices, such as using electrode hosts with two-electron redox centers; replacing the cathode hosts by materials that undergo displacement reactions (e.g. sulfur) by liq. cathodes that may contain flow-through redox mols., or by catalysts for air cathodes; and developing a Li+ solid electrolyte separator membrane that allows an org. and aq. liq. electrolyte on the anode and cathode sides, resp.  Opportunities exist for the chemist to bring together oxide and polymer or graphene chem. in imaginative morphologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqKg_SkQGkZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8OHLWiRUxpg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFSisw%253D%253D&amp;md5=9b3d655905f4b3dc188e98f8370452df</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/35104644"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advances and challenges of sodium ion batteries as post lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sawicki, Monica; Shaw, Leon L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy and climate concerns have made the need for research towards elec. energy storage.  In this context, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant attention lately.  Sodium is an abundant resource that is low cost and safe which makes it an attractive alternative to lithium.  Its chem. properties are similar to that of Li which makes the transition into using Na chem. for ion battery systems feasible.  This review focuses on the latest progress in both cathode and anode materials for SIBs.  It also details research in binders and additives and their effects on the SIB system.  It further highlights the optimization of org. electrolytes and ionic liq. based electrolytes for utilization in SIBs.  The mechanisms of sodium ion storage, transport, and solid electrolyte interphase formation are also discussed to better understand the behavior of ions and battery materials during de/intercalation.  Finally, personal perspectives on outlook and major challenges ahead for SIBs are offered.  These comprehensive and in-depth discussions along with proposed directions can enlighten ideas and offer avenues in the rational design of durable and high performance SIBs in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLbdgNmFLOXLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhpcDqI27rcjw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVSgsL7N&amp;md5=e0f4f5626e9b20b02ef9018ded8ef0bc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSCII</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2374-7951</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhpcDqI27rcjw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703338"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Why is sodium-intercalated graphite unstable?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moriwake, Hiroki; Kuwabara, Akihide; Fisher, Craig A. J.; Ikuhara, Yuichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">58</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">36550-36554</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive low-cost alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Although graphite is used as the neg. electrode in conventional Li-ion batteries, attempts to use it in Na-ion batteries have been hampered by the inability of Na to form graphite intercalation compds. (GICs) under moderate conditions.  It is generally considered that this is due to the size mismatch between Na and the graphite interlayer spacings, but here we show with detailed first-principles calcns. of Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs GICs that the major reason is the change in chem. bonding between alkali metal (AM) ion and C atoms. van der Waals correction terms are introduced to better reproduce the layered graphite structure, and calcd. formation energies of GICs AMC6, where AM = Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs, are found to become less neg. (less stable) as ion size decreases from Cs to Na as a result of weakening ionic bonding, until the formation energy of NaC6 becomes pos.  The much smaller Li ion represents an exception to this trend, as its bonds with C atoms contain a covalent component, resulting in a neg. formation energy.  These subtle differences in staging for the different alkali metals explain why graphite is a good intercalation material for Li and K but not for Na.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpwjr7BwyyKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhpcDqI27rcjw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1Whtr%252FP&amp;md5=a0de324fd0e2a07202566280f0456c52</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/0167-2738(88)90351-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouletier, M.</span></span> <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1988</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28–30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1172</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1175</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0167-2738(88)90351-7</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DGe%252C%2BP.%253B%2BFouletier%252C%2BM.%2BSolid%2BState%2BIonics%2B1988%252C%2B28%25E2%2580%259330%252C%2B1172%25E2%2580%25931175%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2F0167-2738%2888%2990351-7&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="

The PEO-based electrolyte avoids solvent co-intercalation in the electrode material and to a certain extent, reactivity problems due to residual impurities in org. electrolytes.  The amt. of intercalated Na is of the same order of magnitude as in materials prepd. from reaction with Na vapor (NaC64) but smaller than in the compds. synthesized in org. media thus stressing the influence of solvent co-intercalation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpMdmuTxK6ITbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liG3okhUVZ6RQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL1MXmsFKhtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=9a191bcc70365bc329441b7be8a6394b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1149/2.0771608jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Väli, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jänes, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thomberg, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lust, E.</span></span> <i>J.
