                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liG3okhUVZ6RQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2016.09.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene oxide templated nitrogen-doped carbon nanosheets with superior rate capability for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Zhigao; Zhou, Jiang; Cao, Xinxin; Liu, Sainan; Cai, Yangshen; Wang, Lirong; Pan, Anqiang; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">122</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">82-91</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Doping of N atoms into the carbonaceous materials can generate extrinsic defects and more active sites, improve electrode wettability and also broaden the interlayer distance of C, hence promote Na storage capacity and high rate capability.  Herein, the authors report the N-doped C nanosheets materials (PPyCs) obtained from pyrolysis of Polypyrrole coated graphene oxides.  The pyrolysis temp. plays an important role on the electrochem. performance of PPyCs.  With thermal treatment at 400, 600 and 800°, PPyCs have different content of N doping, and the doped N shows different existential forms.  The PPyCs thermal treated at 600° (PPyC-600) exhibit a reversible capacity of 388.8 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, and even at a high c.d. of 10 A g-1, high capacity of 198.6 mA h g-1 is maintained after 10,000 cycles, demonstrating outstanding cyclic stability, and high-rate capability.  Also, the assembled NVP/PPyC-600 full-cell demonstrates a high capacity of 122.2 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles, indicating the practical application of PPyCs nanosheets anode in Na ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzBPgwLJ8sdLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liG3okhUVZ6RQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVGks7vM&amp;md5=066b43a8043a0a062862f7d7e1f6a353</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reduced graphene oxide with superior cycling stability and rate capability for sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yun-Xiao; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising elec. energy storage systems for sustainable energy storage applications due to the abundance of Na and their low cost.  The electrochem. properties of Na ion storage in reduced graphene oxide (RGO) were studied in an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO4 in propylene carbonate (PC).  The RGO anode allowed significant Na ion insertion, leading to higher capacity at high c.d. compared to the previously reported results for C materials.  This is due to the fact that RGO possesses higher elec. cond. and is a more active host, with large interlayer distances and a disordered structure, enabling it to store more Na ions.  RGO anode exhibits high capacity combined with long-term cycling stability at high current densities, leading to reversible capacity ≤174.3 mA-h g-1 at 0.2 C (40 mA g-1), and even 93.3 mA-h g-1 at 1 C (200 mA g-1) after 250 cycles.  Also, RGO could yield a high capacity of 141 mA-h g-1 at 0.2 C (40 mA g-1) over 1000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr39AqQSVabh7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRk4Jm2XelQA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXjtVensLc%253D&amp;md5=704c607778e4fad1ece37d5d579584eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M. LIBs, lithium-ion batteries, owing to high-reversible capacity, high-power capability, good-safety, long-life and zero-memory effects are at the heart of this revolution.  Nonetheless, longer-battery-life, higher-current- and power-d., better-safety, and flexibility, crucial for portables and hybrid-elec.-vehicles further fuel the research to better their electrochem.  Electrode materials are vital for performance of batteries.  Recent developments in nanoscience and nanotechnol. offer potential prospects to devise novel-nanostructured electrode materials for next-generation better-performing LIBs.  Nanostructured materials are pivotal to these progresses due to their manageable surface-area, stunted mass and charge-diffusion span, and vol. change acclimatization during charging/discharging.  CNTs, carbon-nanotubes, with distinct 1D-tubular structure, excellent elec. and thermal conductivities, mech. flexibility and significantly large surface-area, are considered ideal additives to enrich electrodes' chem.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene nanosheets, carbon nanotubes, graphite, and activated carbon as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Xu-Feng; Yang, Cheng-Hsien; Peng, You-Yu; Pu, Nen-Wen; Ger, Ming-Der; Hsieh, Chien-Te; Chang, Jeng-Kuei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. sodium-ion storage properties of graphene nanosheets, carbon nanotubes, mesocarbon microbeads, and activated carbon are investigated.  An irreversible oxidn. occurs for the activated carbon electrode during desodiation, limiting its use in sodium-ion batteries.  The mesocarbon microbead electrode shows a negligible capacity (∼2 mA-h/g), since the graphitic structure has a low surface area and is thus not capable of storing a sufficient amt. of Na+.  In contrast, the carbon nanotube and graphene nanosheet electrodes exhibit reversible capacities of 82 and 220 mA-h/g, resp., at a charge-discharge rate of 30 mA/g.  The high electro-adsorption/desorption area, large no. of Na+ entrance/exit sites, and a large d-spacing of graphene nanosheets contribute to their superior Na+ storage capacity.  At a high rate of 5 A/g, the graphene nanosheet electrode still delivers a capacity of as high as 105 mA-h/g, indicating great high-power ability.  The charge storage mechanism of the electrode is examd. using an ex situ x-ray diffraction technique.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGowDTJBbvo0n7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRk4Jm2XelQA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXmsFeks7k%253D&amp;md5=c2f6190841db243bc12579efa236802f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1021/am302000z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span> <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermo-optical properties of partially unzipped multiwalled carbon nanotubes dispersed nanofluids for direct absorption solar thermal energy systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shende, Rashmi Chandrabhan; Ramaprabhu, S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solar Energy Materials &amp; Solar Cells</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">157</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">117-125</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SEMCEQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0927-0248</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Solar thermal technol. has gained importance during its globalization and industrialization, due to its effective conversion of solar energy into thermal energy.  Common flat black surface solar collectors generally employ selective solid coating as a solar radiation absorber; nevertheless, these collectors have several shortcomings such as low efficiency, corrosion, heating losses and cannot withstand high incident flux.  Theor. and exptl. studies have shown that the nanofluids based Direct Absorber Solar Collectors (DASC) can harvest solar energy more efficiently compared to the conventional solar collectors.  Present work reports the investigation of dispersion stability, optical and thermal properties of partially unzipped multiwalled carbon nanotubes (PUMWNTs) based nanofluids for DASC.  PUMWNTs were prepd. by modified Hummers method.  Nanofluids were prepd. by dispersing calcd. amt. of PUMWNTs in DI water and ethylene glycol (EG).  Absorption and transmittance studies were conducted using UV-vis-NIR spectrophotometer.  Absorption spectra confirmed the stability of nanofluids.  The extinction coeff. of nanofluids shows significant improvement as compared with that of base fluids even at low concn.  Furthermore, the temp. dependent thermal cond. study with different vol. fractions carried out for DI water and EG based nanofluids reported considerable enhancement of 27% and 20.97% resp.  Based on enhanced optical and thermal properties, PUMWNTs dispersed nanofluids are found to be promising to increase the overall efficiency of DASC.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzelUF9CuL4bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljUa7FV4pA_Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xotlerur0%253D&amp;md5=44db4d84d97adb917c46893c0238ef73</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600101"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2559</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2566</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span> <i>Nat.                 10.1038/nmat2612</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DBrezesinski%252C%2BT.%253B%2BWang%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BTolbert%252C%2BS.%2BH.%253B%2BDunn%252C%2BB.%2BNat.%2BMater.%2B2010%252C%2B9%252C%2B146%25E2%2580%2593151%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnmat2612&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous α-MoO3 with iso-oriented nanocrystalline walls for thin-film pseudocapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">146-151</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented α-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the α-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp77bOsasmjcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljUa7FV4pA_Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtVOnt78%253D&amp;md5=b6e743b60b27d6e0dfcabcf281d3d475</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b07132"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12658</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12667</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">CHARMM: The biomolecular simulation program</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brooks, B. R.; Brooks, C. L., III; Mackerell, A. D., Jr.; Nilsson, L.; Petrella, R. J.; Roux, B.; Won, Y.; Archontis, G.; Bartels, C.; Boresch, S.; Caflisch, A.; Caves, L.; Cui, Q.; Dinner, A.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  CHARMM (Chem. at HARvard Mol. Mechanics) is a highly versatile and widely used mol. simulation program.  It has been developed over the last three decades with a primary focus on mols. of biol. interest, including proteins, peptides, lipids, nucleic acids, carbohydrates, and small mol. ligands, as they occur in soln., crystals, and membrane environments.  For the study of such systems, the program provides a large suite of computational tools that include numerous conformational and path sampling methods, free energy estimators, mol. minimization, dynamics, and anal. techniques, and model-building capabilities.  The CHARMM program is applicable to problems involving a much broader class of many-particle systems.  Calcns. with CHARMM can be performed using a no. of different energy functions and models, from mixed quantum mech.-mol. mech. force fields, to all-atom classical potential energy functions with explicit solvent and various boundary conditions, to implicit solvent and membrane models.  The program has been ported to numerous platforms in both serial and parallel architectures.  This article provides an overview of the program as it exists today with an emphasis on developments since the publication of the original CHARMM article in 1983.  © 2009 Wiley Periodicals, Inc. J Comput Chem, 2009.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOXZCtNIqoCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljUa7FV4pA_Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXms1Ciu70%253D&amp;md5=2c6a2be869362d7131f5aea8411c1552</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1016/S0009-2614(03)00741-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaijumon, M.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The SIESTA method for ab initio order-N materials simulation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Soler, Jose M.; Artacho, Emilio; Gale, Julian D.; Garcia, Alberto; Junquera, Javier; Ordejon, Pablo; Sanchez-Portal, Daniel</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2745-2779</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCOMEL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0953-8984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have developed and implemented a selfconsistent d. functional method using std. norm-conserving pseudopotentials and a flexible, numerical LCAO basis set, which includes multiple-zeta and polarization orbitals.  Exchange and correlation are treated with the local spin d. or generalized gradient approxns.  The basis functions and the electron d. are projected on a real-space grid, in order to calc. the Hartree and exchange-correlation potentials and matrix elements, with a no. of operations that scales linearly with the size of the system.  We use a modified energy functional, whose minimization produces orthogonal wavefunctions and the same energy and d. as the Kohn-Sham energy functional, without the need for an explicit orthogonalization.  Addnl., using localized Wannier-like electron wavefunctions allows the computation time and memory required to minimize the energy to also scale linearly with the size of the system.  Forces and stresses are also calcd. efficiently and accurately, thus allowing structural relaxation and mol. dynamics simulations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoylePVm45_4LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIBbPzzboX3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38XivFGrsL4%253D&amp;md5=24e0c50d2d7430790259d11694824ce1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1021/ct5009137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubillus, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubar, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gaus, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rezac, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Elstner, M.</span></span> <i>J. Chem. Theory Comput.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">332</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">342</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ct5009137</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DKubillus%252C%2BM.%253B%2BKubar%252C%2BM.%253B%2BGaus%252C%2BT.%253B%2BRezac%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BElstner%252C%2BM.%2BJ.%2BChem.%2BTheory%2BComput.%2B2015%252C%2B11%252C%2B332%25E2%2580%2593342%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fct5009137&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Parameterization of the DFTB3 Method for Br, Ca, Cl, F, I, K, and Na in Organic and Biological Systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubillus, Maximilian; Kubar, Tomas; Gaus, Michael; Rezac, Jan; Elstner, Marcus</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Chemical Theory and Computation</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">332-342</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCTCCE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1549-9618</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We present an extension to the recent 3OB parametrization of the D. Functional Tight Binding Model DFTB3 for biol. and org. systems.  Parameters for the halogens F, Cl, Br, and I have been developed for use in covalently bound systems and benchmarked on a test set of 106 mols. (the 'OrgX' set), using bonding distances, bonding angles, atomization energies, and vibrational frequencies to assess the performance of the parameters.  Addnl. testing has been done with the X40 set of 40 supramol. systems contg. halogens, adding a simple correction for the halogen bonds that are strongly overbound in DFTB3.  Furthermore, parameters for Ca, K, and Na as counterions in biol. systems have been created.  To benchmark geometries as well as ligand binding energies a test set 'BioMe' of 210 mols. has been created that cover coordination to various functional groups frequently occurring in biol. systems.  The new DFTB3/3OB parameter set outperforms DFT calcns. with a double-ζ basis set in terms of energies and can reproduce DFT geometries, with some minor deviations in bond distances and angles due to the use of a minimal basis set.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3yYWmN4FxGLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIBbPzzboX3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitVKqs7%252FI&amp;md5=2eba8c0d49d4f798f9a4331159afe8a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1021/ct300849w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gaus, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goez, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Elstner, M.</span></span> <i>J. Chem. Theory Comput.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>9</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">338</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">354</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ct300849w</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DGaus%252C%2BM.%253B%2BGoez%252C%2BA.%253B%2BElstner%252C%2BM.%2BJ.%2BChem.%2BTheory%2BComput.%2B2013%252C%2B9%252C%2B338%25E2%2580%2593354%252C%2B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b02275&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fct300849w&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Parametrization and Benchmark of DFTB3 for Organic Molecules</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gaus, Michael; Goez, Albrecht; Elstner, Marcus</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Chemical Theory and Computation</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">338-354</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCTCCE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1549-9618</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">DFTB3 is a recent extension of the self-consistent-charge d.-functional tight-binding method (SCC-DFTB) and derived from a third order expansion of the d. functional theory (DFT) total energy around a given ref. d.  Being applied in combination with the parametrization of its predecessor (MIO), DFTB3 improves for hydrogen binding energies, proton affinities, and hydrogen transfer barriers.  In the present study, parameters esp. designed for DFTB3 are presented, and its performance is evaluated for small org. mols. focusing on thermochem., geometries, and vibrational frequencies from our own and several databases from literature.  The new parameters remove significant overbinding errors, reduce errors for geometries of noncovalent interactions, and improve the overall performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqdzPNg5ShK7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIBbPzzboX3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xhs1ent77L&amp;md5=2ddde03653e32a857d7416e755332515</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2d61f4e775c-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

                10.1021/ja3091438</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Li-Ion Rechargeable Battery: A Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.; Park, Kyu-Sung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1167-1176</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Each cell of a battery stores elec. energy as chem. energy in two electrodes, a reductant (anode) and an oxidant (cathode), sepd. by an electrolyte that transfers the ionic component of the chem. reaction inside the cell and forces the electronic component outside the battery.  The output on discharge is an external electronic current I at a voltage V for a time Δt.  The chem. reaction of a rechargeable battery must be reversible on the application of a charging I and V.  Crit. parameters of a rechargeable battery are safety, d. of energy that can be stored at a specific power input and retrieved at a specific power output, cycle and shelf life, storage efficiency, and cost of fabrication.  Conventional ambient-temp. rechargeable batteries have solid electrodes and a liq. electrolyte.  The pos. electrode (cathode) consists of a host framework into which the mobile (working) cation is inserted reversibly over a finite solid-soln. range.  The solid-soln. range, which is reduced at higher current by the rate of transfer of the working ion across electrode/electrolyte interfaces and within a host, limits the amt. of charge per electrode formula unit that can be transferred over the time Δt = Δt(I).  Moreover, the difference between energies of the LUMO and the HOMO of the electrolyte, i.e., electrolyte window, dets. the max. voltage for a long shelf and cycle life.  The max. stable voltage with an aq. electrolyte is 1.5 V; the Li-ion rechargeable battery uses an org. electrolyte with a larger window, which increase the d. of stored energy for a given Δt.  Anode or cathode electrochem. potentials outside the electrolyte window can increase V, but they require formation of a passivating surface layer that must be permeable to Li+ and capable of adapting rapidly to the changing electrode surface area as the electrode changes vol. during cycling.  A passivating surface layer adds to the impedance of the Li+ transfer across the electrode/electrolyte interface and lowers the cycle life of a battery cell.  Moreover, formation of a passivation layer on the anode robs Li from the cathode irreversibly on an initial charge, further lowering the reversible Δt.  These problems plus the cost of quality control of manufg. plague development of Li-ion rechargeable batteries that can compete with the internal combustion engine for powering elec. cars and that can provide the needed low-cost storage of elec. energy generated by renewable wind and/or solar energy.  Chemists are contributing to incremental improvements of the conventional strategy by investigating and controlling electrode passivation layers, improving the rate of Li+ transfer across electrode/electrolyte interfaces, identifying electrolytes with larger windows while retaining a Li+ cond. σLi &gt; 10-3 S cm-1, synthesizing electrode morphologies that reduce the size of the active particles while pinning them on current collectors of large surface area accessible by the electrolyte, lowering the cost of cell fabrication, designing displacement-reaction anodes of higher capacity that allow a safe, fast charge, and designing alternative cathode hosts.  However, new strategies are needed for batteries that go beyond powering hand-held devices, such as using electrode hosts with two-electron redox centers; replacing the cathode hosts by materials that undergo displacement reactions (e.g. sulfur) by liq. cathodes that may contain flow-through redox mols., or by catalysts for air cathodes; and developing a Li+ solid electrolyte separator membrane that allows an org. and aq. liq. electrolyte on the anode and cathode sides, resp.  Opportunities exist for the chemist to bring together oxide and polymer or graphene chem. in imaginative morphologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqKg_SkQGkZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgklICBeU0N4w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFSisw%253D%253D&amp;md5=9b3d655905f4b3dc188e98f8370452df</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/35104644"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.                         rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Issues and challenges facing rechargeable lithium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tarascon, J.-M.; Armand, M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature (London, United Kingdom)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of the development of lithium-based rechargeable batteries.  Ongoing research strategies are highlighted, and the challenges that remain regarding the synthesis, characterization, electrochem. performance, and safety of these systems are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGowMxv9DmkLwrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIplhUsIMZ4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXovFGitrY%253D&amp;md5=944485672a9bdf09f6e6a7a199bf3d43</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C5RA08321D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sawicki, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaw, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advances and challenges of sodium ion batteries as post lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sawicki, Monica; Shaw, Leon L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">65</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">53129-53154</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy and climate concerns have made the need for research towards elec. energy storage.  In this context, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant attention lately.  Sodium is an abundant resource that is low cost and safe which makes it an attractive alternative to lithium.  Its chem. properties are similar to that of Li which makes the transition into using Na chem. for ion battery systems feasible.  This review focuses on the latest progress in both cathode and anode materials for SIBs.  It also details research in binders and additives and their effects on the SIB system.  It further highlights the optimization of org. electrolytes and ionic liq. based electrolytes for utilization in SIBs.  The mechanisms of sodium ion storage, transport, and solid electrolyte interphase formation are also discussed to better understand the behavior of ions and battery materials during de/intercalation.  Finally, personal perspectives on outlook and major challenges ahead for SIBs are offered.  These comprehensive and in-depth discussions along with proposed directions can enlighten ideas and offer avenues in the rational design of durable and high performance SIBs in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLbdgNmFLOXLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIplhUsIMZ4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVSgsL7N&amp;md5=e0f4f5626e9b20b02ef9018ded8ef0bc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent developments in electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Luyuan Paul; Yu, Linghui; Wang, Xin; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Xu, Zhichuan J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode.  Addnl., opinions on possible solns. through correlating trends in recent papers will be suggested.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaYAOgglkaCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljIplhUsIMZ4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjs1yhsbo%253D&amp;md5=95489f575e9ded7e312db8576036b208</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.                 <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSCII</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2374-7951</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhG1JsXbCXApQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703338"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Why is sodium-intercalated graphite unstable?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moriwake, Hiroki; Kuwabara, Akihide; Fisher, Craig A. J.; Ikuhara, Yuichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">58</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive low-cost alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Although graphite is used as the neg. electrode in conventional Li-ion batteries, attempts to use it in Na-ion batteries have been hampered by the inability of Na to form graphite intercalation compds. (GICs) under moderate conditions.  It is generally considered that this is due to the size mismatch between Na and the graphite interlayer spacings, but here we show with detailed first-principles calcns. of Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs GICs that the major reason is the change in chem. bonding between alkali metal (AM) ion and C atoms. van der Waals correction terms are introduced to better reproduce the layered graphite structure, and calcd. formation energies of GICs AMC6, where AM = Li, Na, K, Rb, and Cs, are found to become less neg. (less stable) as ion size decreases from Cs to Na as a result of weakening ionic bonding, until the formation energy of NaC6 becomes pos.  The much smaller Li ion represents an exception to this trend, as its bonds with C atoms contain a covalent component, resulting in a neg. formation energy.  These subtle differences in staging for the different alkali metals explain why graphite is a good intercalation material for Li and K but not for Na.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpwjr7BwyyKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhG1JsXbCXApQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXht1Whtr%252FP&amp;md5=a0de324fd0e2a07202566280f0456c52</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1016/0167-2738(88)90351-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouletier, M.</span></span> <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1988</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28–30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1172</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1175</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ge, Pascal; Fouletier, Mireille</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. behavior of graphite was studied by using the electrochem. cell: graphite|(polyethylene oxide, CF3SO3Na)|Na.                         rightTab-cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhG1JsXbCXApQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2016.09.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Doping of N atoms into the carbonaceous materials can generate extrinsic defects and more active sites, improve electrode wettability and also broaden the interlayer distance of C, hence promote Na storage capacity and high rate capability.  Herein, the authors report the N-doped C nanosheets materials (PPyCs) obtained from pyrolysis of Polypyrrole coated graphene oxides.  The pyrolysis temp. plays an important role on the electrochem. performance of PPyCs.  With thermal treatment at 400, 600 and 800°, PPyCs have different content of N doping, and the doped N shows different existential forms.  The PPyCs thermal treated at 600° (PPyC-600) exhibit a reversible capacity of 388.8 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, and even at a high c.d. of 10 A g-1, high capacity of 198.6 mA h g-1 is maintained after 10,000 cycles, demonstrating outstanding cyclic stability, and high-rate capability.  Also, the assembled NVP/PPyC-600 full-cell demonstrates a high capacity of 122.2 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles, indicating the practical application of PPyCs nanosheets anode in Na ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzBPgwLJ8sdLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvudtNBlyljA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVGks7vM&amp;md5=066b43a8043a0a062862f7d7e1f6a353</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising elec. energy storage systems for sustainable energy storage applications due to the abundance of Na and their low cost.  The electrochem. properties of Na ion storage in reduced graphene oxide (RGO) were studied in an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO4 in propylene carbonate (PC).  The RGO anode allowed significant Na ion insertion, leading to higher capacity at high c.d. compared to the previously reported results for C materials.  This is due to the fact that RGO possesses higher elec. cond. and is a more active host, with large interlayer distances and a disordered structure, enabling it to store more Na ions.  RGO anode exhibits high capacity combined with long-term cycling stability at high current densities, leading to reversible capacity ≤174.3 mA-h g-1 at 0.2 C (40 mA g-1), and even 93.3 mA-h g-1 at 1 C (200 mA g-1) after 250 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Portable-electronics epitomizing technol. breakthrough in history of mankind, are universal reality thanks to rechargeable batteries.  LIBs, lithium-ion batteries, owing to high-reversible capacity, high-power capability, good-safety, long-life and zero-memory effects are at the heart of this revolution.  Nonetheless, longer-battery-life, higher-current- and power-d., better-safety, and flexibility, crucial for portables and hybrid-elec.-vehicles further fuel the research to better their electrochem.  Electrode materials are vital for performance of batteries.  Recent developments in nanoscience and nanotechnol. offer potential prospects to devise novel-nanostructured electrode materials for next-generation better-performing LIBs.  Nanostructured materials are pivotal to these progresses due to their manageable surface-area, stunted mass and charge-diffusion span, and vol. change acclimatization during charging/discharging.  CNTs, carbon-nanotubes, with distinct 1D-tubular structure, excellent elec. and thermal conductivities, mech. flexibility and significantly large surface-area, are considered ideal additives to enrich electrodes' chem.  Here, we observe contemporary developments in synthesis and characterization of CNTs and CNTs-based nanostructured composite-electrodes for utilization in LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoPnM2h5_brgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvudtNBlyljA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFyitbvO&amp;md5=aebed325d7722f23e2658316acbd55d2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2017.02.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.-Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A SnS2@CNT nanocomposite as a high performance sodium-ion battery anode material is prepd. via a facile sintering route.                         rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene nanosheets, carbon nanotubes, graphite, and activated carbon as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Xu-Feng; Yang, Cheng-Hsien; Peng, You-Yu; Pu, Nen-Wen; Ger, Ming-Der; Hsieh, Chien-Te; Chang, Jeng-Kuei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. sodium-ion storage properties of graphene nanosheets, carbon nanotubes, mesocarbon microbeads, and activated carbon are investigated.  An irreversible oxidn. occurs for the activated carbon electrode during desodiation, limiting its use in sodium-ion batteries.  The mesocarbon microbead electrode shows a negligible capacity (∼2 mA-h/g), since the graphitic structure has a low surface area and is thus not capable of storing a sufficient amt. of Na+.  In contrast, the carbon nanotube and graphene nanosheet electrodes exhibit reversible capacities of 82 and 220 mA-h/g, resp., at a charge-discharge rate of 30 mA/g.  The high electro-adsorption/desorption area, large no. of Na+ entrance/exit sites, and a large d-spacing of graphene nanosheets contribute to their superior Na+ storage capacity.  At a high rate of 5 A/g, the graphene nanosheet electrode still delivers a capacity of as high as 105 mA-h/g, indicating great high-power ability.  The charge storage mechanism of the electrode is examd. using an ex situ x-ray diffraction technique.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGowDTJBbvo0n7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgvudtNBlyljA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXmsFeks7k%253D&amp;md5=c2f6190841db243bc12579efa236802f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/am302000z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span> <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Solar thermal technol. has gained importance during its globalization and industrialization, due to its effective conversion of solar energy into thermal energy.  Common flat black surface solar collectors generally employ selective solid coating as a solar radiation absorber; nevertheless, these collectors have several shortcomings such as low efficiency, corrosion, heating losses and cannot withstand high incident flux.  Theor. and exptl. studies have shown that the nanofluids based Direct Absorber Solar Collectors (DASC) can harvest solar energy more efficiently compared to the conventional solar collectors.  Present work reports the investigation of dispersion stability, optical and thermal properties of partially unzipped multiwalled carbon nanotubes (PUMWNTs) based nanofluids for DASC.  PUMWNTs were prepd. by modified Hummers method.  Nanofluids were prepd. by dispersing calcd. amt. of PUMWNTs in DI water and ethylene glycol (EG).  Absorption and transmittance studies were conducted using UV-vis-NIR spectrophotometer.  Absorption spectra confirmed the stability of nanofluids.  The extinction coeff. of nanofluids shows significant improvement as compared with that of base fluids even at low concn.  Furthermore, the temp. dependent thermal cond. study with different vol. fractions carried out for DI water and EG based nanofluids reported considerable enhancement of 27% and 20.97% resp.  Based on enhanced optical and thermal properties, PUMWNTs dispersed nanofluids are found to be promising to increase the overall efficiency of DASC.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzelUF9CuL4bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhvQXYQRgWdTw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xotlerur0%253D&amp;md5=44db4d84d97adb917c46893c0238ef73</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600101"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2559</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2566</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous α-MoO3 with iso-oriented nanocrystalline walls for thin-film pseudocapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented α-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the α-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp77bOsasmjcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhvQXYQRgWdTw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtVOnt78%253D&amp;md5=b6e743b60b27d6e0dfcabcf281d3d475</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b07132"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12658</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12667</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1545</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/jcc.21287</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">CHARMM: The biomolecular simulation program</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brooks, B. R.; Brooks, C. L., III; Mackerell, A. D., Jr.; Nilsson, L.; Petrella, R. J.; Roux, B.; Won, Y.; Archontis, G.; Bartels, C.; Boresch, S.; Caflisch, A.; Caves, L.; Cui, Q.; Dinner, A. R.; Feig, M.; Fischer, S.; Gao, J.; Hodoscek, M.; Im, W.; Kuczera, K.; Lazaridis, T.; Ma, J.; Ovchinnikov, V.; Paci, E.; Pastor, R. W.; Post, C. B.; Pu, J. Z.; Schaefer, M.; Tidor, B.; Venable, R. M.; Woodcock, H. L.; Wu, X.; Yang, W.; York, D. M.; Karplus, M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Computational Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1545-1614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCCHDD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0192-8651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  CHARMM (Chem. at HARvard Mol. Mechanics) is a highly versatile and widely used mol. simulation program.  It has been developed over the last three decades with a primary focus on mols. of biol. interest, including proteins, peptides, lipids, nucleic acids, carbohydrates, and small mol. ligands, as they occur in soln., crystals, and membrane environments.  For the study of such systems, the program provides a large suite of computational tools that include numerous conformational and path sampling methods, free energy estimators, mol. minimization, dynamics, and anal. techniques, and model-building capabilities.  The CHARMM program is applicable to problems involving a much broader class of many-particle systems.  Calcns. with CHARMM can be performed using a no. of different energy functions and models, from mixed quantum mech.-mol. mech. force fields, to all-atom classical potential energy functions with explicit solvent and various boundary conditions, to implicit solvent and membrane models.  The program has been ported to numerous platforms in both serial and parallel architectures.  This article provides an overview of the program as it exists today with an emphasis on developments since the publication of the original CHARMM article in 1983.  © 2009 Wiley Periodicals, Inc. J Comput Chem, 2009.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOXZCtNIqoCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhvQXYQRgWdTw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXms1Ciu70%253D&amp;md5=2c6a2be869362d7131f5aea8411c1552</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1016/S0009-2614(03)00741-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaijumon, M.                             rightTab-cas_R
